PREFACE.
This volume has been prepared in accordance with the provisions of Section
6 (1) ?f ~he Census Act (Northern Ireland), 1~36, from the census returns made as
at :0:1dn1ght of the 28th February-1st March,· 1937.
The statistics contained
herein supersede those contained in the Preliminary Report published in July,
1937, and may be regarded as final.
The census which was taken in 1937 vvas a special one in the sense that it was
limited _in _scope and was taken during the interval betvveen normal census years,
thus bndg1ng the gap between the 1926 census and the next full census.
Certain
ques~ions usually found on census schedules, such as those regarding occupation
and Industry, \Vere not asked, but a question as to usual residence was in-
cluded for the first time.
The 1937 census publications will consist of:-
1.
SEVEN CouNTY VoLUMES, each similar in design and scope.
The smallest
area for which statistics are given in the county volu1nes is the district
electoral division, but statistics in respect of townlands, similar to
those given in table 4, have been prepared in the Census Office and
are available for reference upon application to the Registrar General.
2.
GENERAL REPORT for the whole country, covering in more detail the
statistics sho-wn in the county volumes, and, in addition, containing
tables on fertility.
3.
SUPPLEMENT TO 1926 TOPOGRAPHICAL INDEX.
This supplement will be
designed for use in conjunction with the 1926 topographical index in
order to facilitate reference to the statistics given in the 1937 census
reports. It will cover the topographical changes during the intercensal
period.
The net prices for the 1937 census publications will be 2s. 6d. for each county
volume, 5s. Od. for the general report and Is. Od. for the topographical index
supplement.
The volumes for Belfast County Borough, County Antrim, and County Armagh
have already been published. The remaining county volumes will be published
~at intervals as completed, and the other two volumes as soon as possible thereafter.
These publications will be obtainable directly from the Superintendent, H.M.
Stationery Office, 80 Chichester Street, Belfast, or through any bookseller.
The 1926 census publications are similar in arrangement to the 1937 series
(see advertisement on back of cover), but they incorporate additional tables on
occupations, industries, education, orphanhood, dependent children, infirmities,
and nationality. In addition the 1926 county volumes contain population and
housing figures for each townland.
The Registrar General is empo·wered by Section 6 (2) of the Census Act
(Northern Ireland), 1936, to prepare and supply from the census returns statistics
other than those published in the census reports at the reqpest and cost of any
local authority or person.
Any special demands for statistics more detailed than
those published (either as regards subject matter or area) can thus, as far as office
records permit, be satisfied.
Requests for special compilation of census statistics
should be addressed to the Registrar General, who will be pleased to advise and
furnish an estimate of cost.
·
MINISTRY OF FINANCE,
OCTOBER, 1938.
GENERAL REGISTER OFFICE,
FERMANAGH HOUSE,
0RMEAU AVENUE,
BELFAST.

EXPLANATORY NOTES AND DEFINITIONS OF
TERMS USED IN THIS VOLUME.
AREA.
The acreages given are as measured by the Ordnance Survey of 1919-1920
(foreshore, as \Veil as the areas of Carlingford and Strangford Laughs, being
excluded), and relate to the several administrative areas as constituted at the date
of the 1937 census.
The acreage covered by the larger rivers, lakes, and tideways

    is not included in the body of tables 4, 6, and 7, but is given in footnotes thereto.
POPULATION.
The population comprises all persons, including Forces of the Crown, enumerated
as at 1nidnight of Sunday, 28th February-1st March, 1937.
The population assigned to each area comprises all persons who were actually
present within it, whether as residents or visitors, persons on board ship in port being
included in the area adjacent to the port.
In general these definitions apply to the population figures for previous census
enumerations vvhich have been included in some of the tables.
ADMINISTRATIVE DIVISIONS.
The County is divided into eighteen county districts (one municipal borough,
nine urban districts and eight rural districts), comprising 146 district electoral
divisions, twenty-t\vo of which are coterminous with wards of the municipal borough
or urban districts, or entire urban districts where these areas are not divided into
\vards (see table 4).
The district electoral division forms the unit for the purpose
of poor law elections.
Eight poor law unions are contained within the County-three wholly and
five partly; the remaining portions of the divided unions being situated in Antrim
and Armagh Counties and Belfast County Borough.
The medical dispensary
districts situat~d in the County number thirty-six (see table 6).
Poor law unions .
and medical dispensary districts are, respectively, superintendent registrars' and
registrars' districts for tne registration of marriages, births and deaths.
There are eight parliamentary constituencies for elections to the Northern
Ireland Parliament, and one ·for elections to the United Kingdom Parliament (see
table 12).
The County is divided into twenty county electoral divisions, which are the
units for the purpose of county council elections (see table 7).
The County forms a single regional education area under the authority of
the County Council.
The changes made in the constitution or boundaries of administrative divisions
of the County during the last intercensal period are as follows:-
(a) Bangor Urban District was made a Municipal Borough (23rd January,
1928) ;
(b) The North and South Wards of Newry Urban District were reconstituted
(17th April, 1928), and consequential changes were made to the district
electoral divisions affected ;
(c) A minor adjustment, involving roadway only, was made in the boundary
between Warrenpoint Urban District and Newry No. l Rural District
(1st April, 1930) ;
(d) Kilkeel was made a town under the Towns Improvement (Ireland) Act,
1854 (1st August, 1934) ;
(e) The creation by the House of Commons (Method of Voting and Redistribu~
tion of Seats) Act (Northern Ireland), 1929, of eight parliamentary con-
stituencies for the purpose of elections to the Parliament of Northern
Ireland.
·
HOUSES.
1.
The term '' House '' denotes
(a) each _structure containin&" dwelling accommodation (except institutions)
whrch stands alone or 1s separated from another by a vertical parting
wall;


---

## Page 7

vii
(b) each institution (viz., hospital, sanatorium, nursing home, infirmary,
workhouse, asylum, religious or charitable community, residential school,
barracks, or other premises under Naval, Military, Air Force, or Police
discipline, prison or other place of detention), regardless of the number
of buildings within its precincts ;
(c) each vessel, caravan, tent, or other movable d\velling.
2.
The term " Private House " denotes any " House " other than an institu-
tion, hotel, boarding house, premises occupied by caretaker, vessel, movable
dwelling, or barn, stable, or out-house occupied on census night.
The term
includes premises comprising a shop and dwelling, unless occupied by a caretaker.
3.
The terms " Boarding House " and " Lodging House " cover any habitation
described as such, and any private house containing three or more boarders or
lodgers.
4.
The term " Inhabited " denotes actual residence of a person or persons
on census night. "Uninhabited" houses, therefore, include those temporarily
vacant through the absence of the usual occupiers as well as those \Vithout tenants.
Enumerators were instructed to omit from their enumeration empty houses in
such an advanced state of decay that there was little likelihood that they would again
be made fit for habitation.
ROOMS.
L
The rooms enumerated are the usual living rooms, including bedrooms
and kitchens, but excluding sculleries (i.e. rooms used only for washing, cleaning,
or cooking), pantries, cloakrooms, landings, closets or bathrooms, or any ware-
house, office or shop rooms.
2.
For the purpose of classifying families by rooms occupied (table 10),
shared-room occupations-i.e., those in which two or more families have rooms
in common-have been rounded up to the next integral number of rooms.
Thus,
three families occupying It, 2!, and 2t rooms in a 6-roomed house would be
shown as occupying 2, 3, and 3 rooms respectively.
The extent of the rounding-
up process may be ascertained in a general way by refer.ence to table 9, in which
the actual number of rooms is given.
In cases of l room occupations, all persons
living therein, whether related or not, have been enumerated as one census family.
FAMILIES.
The term " Family " denotes
(a) The inhabitants of each institution (see 1 (b) of " Houses " definition),
hotel, boarding-house, lodging-house, or ship ;
(b) Each person or group of persons occupying separately the whole, or part
of, any dwelling other than as described at (a).
" Private Family " means a family enumerated in a " Private House " (see 2
of " Houses" definition).
It should be specially noted that a family or private family for census purposes
includes any group of persons, whether related or not,. who live together in·
separate house or room accommodation.
RATEABLE VALUATION.
The figures shown are those fo..r service of the year ended 31st March, 1937
and have been supplied by the Commissioner of Valuation.
Rateable ~eredita­
ments include lands, buildings, tolls, railways, tramways, canals, fishenes, tur-
. baries, mines, quarries, waterworks, telegraphs, telephones, and electricity, gas, and
water mains.
Subject to the provisions of the Local Governme:r:-t (Rating 3:n~ Finance) Act
(Northern Ireland), 1929, agricultural land and agricultural buildings are com-
pletely derated and industrial hereditame~ts an~ freight:transport here~itaments
are partially derated, the consequent deficiency In the yield of rates be1ng made
good by contributions from the Government.


---

## Page 8

viii
The figures in tables 4, 6, and 7 are the totals of the valuations of the .rateable
hereditan1ents in the various areas before reduction on account of derating, but
exclude in certain cases the valuations of electricity, gas, and water mains, telegraphs,
telephones, fisheries, waterworks, and tramways. The total of such excluded
valuations for the County is given in a footnote to the tables.
The total for th~ County of rateable valuations after reduction on account of
derating is given on page xi.
USUAL RESIDENCE ..
The persons who have been regarded as having their usual residence in the
area of enumeration (i.e., municipal borough, urban district or rural district)
include:-
(1) Those enumerated in their own houses.
(2) Persons having settled residence in private lodgings.
(3) Resident domestic servants.
(4) Scholars, students, etc., living during term time in residential schools, etc.
(5) Inmates of institutions when the nature of the institution suggested per-
manent residence.
(6) Crews of vessels if usually living on shipboard.
(7) Persons whose usual residence, although not in the building in which
enumerated, is still within the area of enumeration.
All other persons have been treated as usually resident in urban or rural areas
other than the area of enumeration, or in countries outside Northern Ireland, as
stated on the census schedule.
BIRTHPLACES.
In the case of a very small number of persons whose birthplaces were not
stated on the census schedule they were assumed to have been born in the county
in which enumerated, except when the person bore a foreign name, or was stated
to be a visitor to the household.
After further enquiry in the latter cases it was
found possible to classify them under definite headings.
RELIGIONS.
The classification of religions includes a separate heading for each religion, the
adherents of which number 10 or more (see table 20). The heading" Presbyterian)}
includes those who call themselves by this name without qualification, together with
those who describe themselves as members of the Church of Scotland.
Other Presby-
terian denominations are shown at the foot of table 20.
Members of the Church of
England and the Episcopal Church of Scotland have been included under the heading
'' Church of Ireland.''
The latter group includes all persons returned as '' Church ''
or " Episcopalian."
NUMBERING OF TABLES.
Roman numerals are used in this volume for the tables in Part I and Arabic
numerals for the tables in Part II.
CONVENTION AS TO DOTS (.) AND DASHES (-).
Throughout this volume dots (.) are used in-tabular matter to indicate "none,"
and dashes (-) are used to indicate that information cannot or is not intended to
be given.
CORRECTION OF CENSUS DATA.
Every effort has been made to eliminate, as far as practicable, errors and
inaccuracies from the census data.
The process of correction began with the
enumerators, who received full instructions as to the action to be taken in cases
of error or omission, and it was continued by a system of check and further enquiry
where necessary applied at each stage of the office work. The process was com-
pleted by an intensive scrutiny and inter-comparison of the tabulation sheets.
This scrutiny and comparison revealed a certain number of incongruities, which,
when material, were investigated and corrected in the light of the facts disclosed.
In a few cases trifling errors were adjusted arbitrarily.

PART I.
STATISTICAL NOTES.
NoTES.-(1) The definitions, etc., contairz,ed in the preceding section apply
equally to these notes and to the tables in Pa·rt II.
(2) The descriptions of areas ~used in this vohtn~e relate to the cot~­
ditions which existed at the census date.
In particular, the ter1n Irish Free State has
been employed in the tables, since the na1ne Eire did 1zot con~ze into operatio·n for that
area until the 29th December, 1937.
AREA.
The area of Down County, exclusive of the larger rivers, lakes, and tide·ways,

    is 952 square miles or 18.2 per cent. of the area of Northern Ireland. In 1896 and
1899 the County lost an aggregate of almost five square miles to Belfast County
Borough and Antrim County, but gained in the latter year over half a square mile
from Armagh County (see note to table 2).
POPULATION.
The total population enumerated in the County was 210,687.
This is the
highest number recorded since 1891, and represents an increase of 1,459, or 0.7
per cent., in the population during the past eleven years.
(Figures for census
years 1821 onwards are shown in table 2).

* Owing to the fact that prior to 1899, certain registration units in Belfast Union extended over both the
urban and rural portions thereof, it is not practicable to show figures in this table for periods prior to 1901.
t Including the deaths of non-civilians of the County of Down which occurred outside Northern Ireland.
Comparable figures of natural increase due to the excess of births over deaths,
in respect of the County as now constituted, are not available for periods prior to

    1901, but it is evident that in each of the intercensal periods from 1841 to 1901, as
well as from 1901 to 1911, there was a net outward movement of such dimensions
as not only to counterbalance the natural increase, but to cause an actual decrease
in population. Since 1911, however, the net outward movement was of smaller
dimensions and only partially offset the increase which would otherwise have re-
sulted from the excess of births over deaths. There can be little doubt that during
the last century the net movement outwards has to a considerable extent been
due to movement into Belfast as well as to the extension of the County Borough
boundary.

Municipal Borough, and Urban and Rural Districts.-All urban areas,
with the exception of Donaghadee and Dromore, show increases in population
. during the past eleven years J and all rural areas, except Castlereagh and Hills-
borough, show decreases.
In most cases the increase or decrease is a continuation
of the trend since 1901.
The 1937 urban population constitutes 29.7 per cent. of

    the whole as compared with 27.5 per cent. in1926, 22.9 per cent. in 1911, and 19.6
per cent. in 190l~the percentages quoted relating to urban areas as constituted
at the date of each census. It ~s of interest to note that the greatest absolute
increase in population was recorded in the Borough of Bangor ; and that the
greatest relative increase occurred in Castlereagh Rural District1 -vvhich adjoins
Belfast. The greatest absolute and relative decreases occurred in the Rural
Districts of Downpatrick and Moira, respectively.
·
The persons per acre rates vary in the urban areas between 22.2 in Newtown-
ards and 1.9 in Newcastle. These rates, however, should be used \Vith reserve in
measuring the density of population in urban areas, since the acreages of these
areas may include widely differing proportions of waste land, land under pasture,
etc. In rural districts the rates vary betvveen 0.7 and 0.2 persons per acre.
Table 3 shows the extent to which each urban and rural area in the County
has lost or gained by migration during the intercensal period.
Nearly one half of
the increase in the femalt? population of urban areas as a whole is due to· migration.


---

## Page 11

xi
Tables 4 provides more detailed information regarding urban and rural areas
and their constituent district electoral divisions, including figures of rateable valuation.
The rateable valuation of the County in 1937 was £1,236,097 before reduction on
account of derating, and £706,100 after reduction on account of derating.
These
figures compare with a rateable valuation of £893,941 in 1926, but it is to be noted
that during the intercensal period, not only \Vas a derating scheme introduced,
but a general revaluation took place.
Towns and Villages other than Urban Districts.-Table 5 deals with
towns created under the Towns Improvement (Ireland) Act, 1854, and towns or
villages ·which may be defined as clusters of 50 or more houses with no legally
defined boundaries. The total number of these two classes of to·wns is 55, and of
these, two have a population of over 2,000 each, six have a population of between

    1,000 and 2,000 each, and fifteen have a population of between 500 and 1,000 each.
The total population .of the 55 towns is 33,423.
Unions and Dispensary Districts.-Detailed figures for these areas are given
in table 6.
County Electoral Divisions .-Detailed figures for these, together with the
reference numbers of the district electoral divisions comprised in each, are given in
table 7.
Parliamentary Constituencies.-A complete record of the parliamentary
constituencies of the County for elections to both the United Kingdom and Northern
Ireland Parliaments is set out in table 12. The number of electors as shown therein
is in accordance with the register which came into force on the 15th December, 1937.
This register was compiled on the basis of the qualifying period of six months ending
on 15th July, 1937, and therefore approximates in point of time to the census date.
In general a person is entitled to be registered as a Northern Ireland parlia-
mentary elector if he or she is twenty-one or more years of age, and
(a) has the requisite residence qualification of six months and (unless a native
of Northern Ireland) has been resident in the United Kingdom for seven
years prior to the qualifying date; or
·
(b) has occupied for six months prior to the qualifying date business premises
of at least £10 valuation ; or
(c) is the wife or husband of a person entitled to be registered in respect of a
business premises qualification.
Graduates of full age form the electorate of the constituency of Queen's
University, Belfast.
The qualifications for voters at elections for the return of members to serve in
the parliament at Westminster are identical with those set out above, except that
the overriding provision as to residence in the United Kingdom does not apply.
In considering the number of electors as shown on the register it should be
noted that a person's name may appear on the register once only on a residence
qualification, but any number of times on a business premises qualification, pro-
vided the residence and business premises are all situated in different constituencies.
Thus a person having a business premises vote in each of two constituencies
for Northern Ireland elections would only have one business premises vote for United
Kingdom elections if both business premises were situated in the same United King-
dom constituency.

Education Area~-The Down County Council is the education authority
for the entire County, which forms a single regional education area, and all figures
given in this volume for the County therefore apply equally to the Down Regional
Education Area.
HOUSING-BUILDINGS, ROOMS, FAMILIES, Etc.

including institutions, etc., which are normally used for dv,relling purposes, and,
since nearly 99 per cent. of these are houses containing private dwellings, the com-
parisons afforded by the tables can be regarded as being broadly applicable to
housing of private census families.

The intercensal increase of 6,320 houses is equivalent to an increase of a little
over 12 per cent. as compared with an increase in population during the eleven
years of less than I per cent.
The number of uninhabited houses has incre.ased materially since 1926, but,
compared with the numbers recorded at the three preceding censuses, the 1937
figure is not abnormally high.
·
Persons per Inhabited House and Persons per Census Family.-As
might have been expected, the average number of persons per house, which has

    been steadily declining from 1851, shows a further decrease from 1926 to 1937.
The size of the census family, which shows a corresponding decline, is now just
over four persons per family.
As compared With an increase of less than 1 per
cent. in the population since 1926, the number of census families has increased by
nearly 8 per cep.t.
··
Ratios for persons per house, persons per family, and persons per room in
relation to inhabited private houses only, can readily be calculated for each county
district from tables 8 and 10.
A comparison of these ratios for 1926 and 1937
in respect of the County is as follows :-

(a) The figures given are for the County Districts as constituted at the date of the 1937 Census.
There have been increases in the total number of houses in all county districts
with the exceptions of the Rural Districts of Banbridge, Moira and Newry No. l,
where small decreases have been recorded.
Generally, the increases are most
marked in urban areas on the coast and in rural areas adjoining, or within easy
reach of, Belfast, thus follo·wing the trend exhibited at the 1926 census. The gains
in Castlereagh Rural District, Newcastle Urban District, and Bangor Municipal

    Borough-45.3 per cent., 38.1 per cent., and 37.1 per cent. respectively-are
specially noteworthy.
Table VII has been prepared from tables 9 and 10, and shows broadly the
distribution, by size, of private houses and the degree of subletting in the County.
It also shows the distribution of private families according to the number of rooms
occupied by them.
Similar particulars in regard to each county district can readily
be obtained from tables 9 and 10 in this volume and corresp~nding tables in the ·
Down County Report for the 1926 census.

For the County as a whole substantial increases are recorded in the proportion
of 4 and 5 roomed houses and in the proportion of families in dwelling accommoda-
tion containing the same number of rooms.
These increases are shared by all the
county districts.
The number of inhabited 1 to 3 roomed houses in all districts shows a
marked decrease as compared with the number in 1926.
The proportion of private houses sublet in 1937 was 0.5 per cent., which rate
is only slightly more than one-half of the rate for 1926.

Overcrowding.-In order to determine the measure of accommodation
essential if overcrowding is to be avoided, a knowledge of many factors such as
size of family, number, ages, and sex of children, and number and size of rooms
is necessary.
Moreover, it is a matter of individual judgment as to what weight
should be given to each factor.
In dealing with the accommodation particulars
recorded at the census, the size of the census family has been expressed by the
number of per?ons, without regard to sex or age, and the rooms comprising the
accommodation have been limited to the usual living rooms.
The resulting rates
of persons per room have been regarded as an approximate measure of density
of room accommodation, and a rate of over 2 persons per room has been assumed
to indicate overcrowding.

Tables VIII and IX have been prepared from table 10.
The first of these
tables shows a marked general improvement in accommodation in the County as
a whole, and table IX shows that all county districts share in this improvement.
The actual amount of overcrowding i~ the various districts is s4own by the
population numbers and the relative degree of overcrowding is indicated by the
density rates in the category of "over 2 persons per room."
Housing in Dispensary Districts .-Table 6 contains detailed housing and
accommodation figures for dispensary districts, which show increases in the number
of houses in 30 out of the 36 districts.
The greatest relative increases are among
the districts in Belfast, Lisburn and Newtownards Unions.

Private Houses
..
The total number of hotels and boarding houses in the County has decreased
by 39 per cent. and their population by 44 per cent. This decrease is no doubt,
to some extent, due to the additional houses built since 1926, ~hich must have
absorbed many people hitherto living in boarding houses as well as persons who
previously occupied portions of houses.
Half of the hotels and boarding houses
in the County are located in urban areas on the coast, but it will be appreciated
that in addition to the premises described in the census returns as hotels and
boarding houses a considerable number of private houses in coastal districts are
used for the accommodation of visitors during the summer season.
USUAL RESIDENCE.
The new enquiry at this census as to the "usual residence" of each individual
irrespective of the address at which he or she was enumerated has disclosed that
only 1.5 per cent. of the enumerated population of Down County was normally
resident elsewhere than in the urban or rural area of enumeration, and nearly
one quarter of these resided outside Northern Ireland.
Detailed figures are
contained in table 13.
This table shows the effect of an adjustment of
the enumerated population for usual residence as between the urban or rural
area of enumeration and other such areas in Northern Ireland.
While this ·
adjustment is comparatively insignificant, it may be noted that it would
probably have been larger if the census had been taken some months later. More-
over, the 1937 population figures adjusted for usual residence as at the end of
February will provide better comparisons with population figures similarly adjusted
at a future census (which may be taken at some other time of the year) than
simple comparisons of populations as enumerated. In the absence of particulars
of persons not enumerated in Northern Ireland, but usually resident in the County,
it is not possible to make a similar adjustment for usual residence as between areas
in the County and places outside Northern Ireland.
If, powever, a census
enumeration were carried out on the same date throughout the· British Isles, it
could be made to provide most of the particulars required to give effect to such
an adjustment.
· ·

Table 16 shows the population of each urban and rural area by sex, marital
condition, and quinquennial groups of age.
This table has been prepared on lines
similar to those adopted in England and Wales, and will facilitate the study of
questions of fertility. It will be seen that the number of females exceeds the
number of males in each urban area and in the rural districts of Banbridge, Hills-
borough, Moira, and Ne\vtownards.
The population of the aggregates of urban
and rural areas, by sex, 1narital condition, and quinquennial groups of age, is given
in Table 15.
Table 17 shows for the County as a whole, and also for each urban and rural
area, the population under age twenty-one by sex and individual ages.
The
numbers are also divided between Roman Catholic and other denominations.
A
similar division by religions takes place among children at the school
ages,
during which there is
almost
complete separation between the
two groups.
This. separation is one of a number of social differences between
Roman Catholics and others, and these differences have a considerable statistical
significance.
For this reason, therefore, in this and certain of the following tables
separate figures for Roman Catholics and others have been shown.

table does show that, taken together, the county districts ·with the highest
proportion of Roman Catholic population have a higher ratio of young children
to married women than the remaining county districts.

BIRTHPLACES.
Table 18 shows, by sex, the numbers of persons born in each of the counties
of Northern Ireland, other countries in the British Isles, overseas don1inions,
colonies, etc., foreign countries, and at sea.
Of the total population of Down
County, 75 percent. were born in the County, St per cent. in Belfast County Borough,
8 per cent. in the other five counties of Northern Ireland, and nearly 4 per
cent. in the Irish Free State.
Only 0. 7 per cent. were born outside the British
Isles, and almost one half of these were born in British Dominions and Colonies.
Persons born in foreign countries nun1bered 819, and ll persons were born at sea.

From table XVIII it will be seen that from 1861 to 1926 the general tendency
was for the proportions born in Northern Ireland to fall and the proportions born
in other parts of the British Isles and abroad to rise. This tendency \vas, ho\vever,
checked in the last intercensal period when there was a reduction in the proportions
born in Great Britain.
Table 19 deals vvith the numbers of persons in each county district born
outside Northern Ireland and shows them under Roman Catholic and other
denominations, by sex, and duration of residence in Northern Ireland. The
irregularities in the run of the figures as bet\veen males and females in some
cases are no doubt due to circumstances peculiar to particular districts.
Districts with a high proportion of domestic servants, and those ·which include
schools, institutions, etc., for example, might be expected to sho\v some exceptional
features.

Table XIX has been prepared from table 19 and sho\vs under Rorp.an Catholic
and other denominations the numbers of persons who have come from
other countries to reside in the County, and were still there on census day. It will
be seen that the ratio of such persons to the total population of the same denon1ina-
tion for all periods of residence compares closely as between Roman Catholics and
persons of other denominations. The figures, therefore, do not suggest that in
recent years there has been any disproportionate influx of persons, either of Roman
Catholic or other denominations, fron1 other countries into the County, unless
any such influx has been offset by a corresponding movement outwards from the
County.

    t Including Church of England, and Episcopal Church of Scotland, numbering 2,466 and 1, respectively, in 1937.
From table XX it will be seen that increased numbers and percentages have
been recorded for Roman Catholics, Methodists, and persons of other denominations
while Presbyterians and Church of Ireland show decreases both in numbers and
percentages.
From 1861 onwards the general tendency has been for the pro-
portion of Presbyterians to decrease and for those of Methodists and persons of
other denominations to increase. The proportions for Roman Catholics and
Church of Ireland have only fluctuated between narrovv limits during this period.

2. The figures given are for the County as constituted at the date of each Census, exclusive of

a County Borough in 1898.
The boundaries of the County were further altered, pursuant
to the Local Government (Ireland) Act, 1898, by
(a) the transfer to County Antrim of the portion of Lisburn Urban District formerly
situated in County Down, and
(b) the transfer from County Armagh of the portion of Newry Urban District formerly
situated in that County.

## Page 67

111
PREFACE.
This volume has been prepared in accordance with the provisions of Section
6 (1) of the Census Act (Northern Ireland), 1936, from the census returns made as
at midnight of the 28th February-1st March, 1937.
The statistics contained
herein supersede those contained in the Preliminary Report published in July,
1937, and may be regarded as final.
The census which was taken in 1937 was a special one in the· sense that it was
limited in scope and was taken during the interval between normal census years,
thus bridging the gap between the 1926 census and the next full census.
Certain
questions usually found on census schedules, such as those regarding occupation
and industry, were not asked, but a question as to usual residence was in-
cluded for the first time.
The 1937 census publications will consist of :-
1.
SEVEN CouNTY VoLUMES, each similar in design and scope.
The smallest
area for which statistics are given in the county volumes is the district
electoral division, but statistics in respect of townlands, similar to
those given in table 4, have been prepared in the Census Office and
are available for reference upon application to the Registrar General.
2.
GENERAL REPORT for the whole country, covering in more detail the
statistics shown in the county volumes, and, in addition, containing
tables on fertility.
3.
SuPPLEMENT TO 1926 ToPOGRAPHICAL INDEX.
This supplement will be
designed for use in conjunction with the 1926 topographical index in
order to facilitate reference to the statistics given in the 1937 census
reports. It will cover the topographical changes during the intercensal
period.
The net prices for the 1937 census publications will be 2s. 6d. for each county
volume, 5s. Od. for the general report and 1s. Od. for the topographical index
supplement.
The volumes for Belfast County Borough and Counties Antrim, Armagh,
Down, and Fermanagh have already been published. The volume for County
Londonderry will be published shortly, and the other two volumes as soon as
possible thereafter. These publications will be obtainable directly from the
Superintendent, H. M. Stationery Office, 80 Chichester Street, Belfast, or through
any bookseller.
The 1926 census publications are similar in arrangement to the 1937 series
(see advertisement on back of cover), but they incorporate additional tables on
occupations, industries, education, orphanhood, dependent children, infirmities,
and nationality. In addition the 1926 county volumes contain population and
housing figures for each townland.
The Registrar General is empowered by Section 6 (2) of the Census Act
(Northern Ireland), 1936, to prepare and supply from the census returns statistics
other than those published in the census reports at the request and cost of any
local authority or person.
Any special demands for statistics more detailed than
those published (either as regards subject matter or area) can thus, as far as office
records permit, be satisfied.
Requests for special compilation of census statistics
should be addressed to the Registrar General, who will be pleased to advise and
furnish an estimate of cost.
NovEMBER, 1938.
MINISTRY OF FINANCE,
GENERAL REGISTER OFFICE,
FERMANAGH HoUSE,
0RMEAU AVENUE,
BELFAST.

## Page 70

EXPLANATORY NOTES AND DEFINITIONS OF
TERMS USED IN THIS VOLUME.
AREA.
The acreages given are as measured by the Ordnance Survey of 1899-1907
(foreshore being excluded), and relate to the several administrative areas as
constituted at the date of the 1937 census.
The acreage covered by the larger

    rivers and lakes is not included in the body of tables 4, 6, and 7, but is given in
footnotes thereto.
POPULATION.
The population comprises all persons, including Forces of the Crown, enumerated
as at midnight of Sunday, 28th February-1st March, 1937.
The population assigned to each area comprises all persons who were actually
present within it, whether as residents or visitors.
In general these definitions apply to the population figures for previous census
enumerations which have been included in some of the tables.
ADMINISTRATIVE DIVISIONS.
The County is divided into ten county districts (four urban districts and six
rural districts), comprising 145 district electoral divisions, eleven of which are
coterminous with entire urban districts or wards thereof (see table 4).
The district
electoral division forms the unit for the purpose of poor law elections.
Seven poor law unions are contained within the County-six wholly and one
partly, the remaining portion of the divided union being situated in County
F ermanagh. The medical dispensary districts situated in the County number
twenty-eight (see table 6).
Poor law unions and medical dispensary districts are,
respectively, superintendent registrars' and registrars' districts for the registration
of marriages, births and deaths.
There are :five parliamentary constituencies for elections to the Northern
Ireland Parliament.
For the purpose of elections to the United Kingdom Parlia-
ment Counties Tyrone and Fermanagh together fonn a single constituency (see
table 12).
The County is divided into twenty-seven county electoral divisions, which
are the units for the purpose of county council elections (see table 7).
The County Council forms an education authority for the County, and its
powers and duties are exercised through five regional education committees, whose
areas each comprise one or more urban or rural districts (see page xii).
The changes made in the constitution or boundaries of administrative divisions
of the County during the last intercensal period are as follows :-
(a) The three Wards of Omagh Urban District, viz., the North, South, and
West Wards, were reconstituted (25th June, 1935), and the boundaries
of the three existing District Electoral Divisions similarly named were
made coterminous with those of the Wards as reconstituted (26th
June, 1935) ;
·
(b) The creation by the House of Commons (Method of Voting and Redistri-
bution of Seats) Act (Northern Ireland), 1929, of five parliamentary
constituencies for the purpose of elections to the Parliament of Northern
Ireland.
HOUSES.
1.
The term '' House '' denotes
(a) each structure containing dwelling accommodation (except institutions)
which stands alone or is separated from another by a vertical parting
wall;


---

## Page 71

vii
(b) each institution (viz., hospital, sanatorium, nursing home, infirmary,
workhouse, asylum, religious or charitable community, residential school,
barracks, or other premises under Naval, Military, Air Force, or Police
discipline, prison or other place of detention), regardless of the number
of buildings within its precincts;
(c) each vessel, caravan, tent, or other movable dwelling.
2.
The term" Private House" denotes any" House" other than an institu-
tion, hotel, boarding house, premises occupied by caretaker, vessel, movable
dwelling, or barn, stable, or out-house occupied on census night. The term
includes premises comprising a shop and dwelling, unless occupied by a caretaker.
3.
The terms " Boarding House" and " Lodging House" cover any habitation
described as such, and any private house containing three or more boarders or
lodgers.
4.
The term '' Inhabited '' denotes actual residence of a person or persons
on census night.
" Uninhabited " houses, therefore, include . those temporarily
vacant through the absence of the usual occupiers as well as those without tenants.
Enumerators were instructed to omit from their enumeration empty houses in
such an advanced state of decay that there was little likelihood that they would again
be made fit for habitation.
ROOMS.
1.
The rooms enumerated are the usual living roo1ns, including bedrooms
and kitchens, but excluding sculleries (i.e. rooms used only for washing, cleaning,
or cooking), pantries, cloakrooms, landings, closets or bathrooms, or any ware-
house, office or shop rooms.
2.
For the purpose of classifying families by rooms occupied (table 10),
shared-room occupations-i.e., those in which two or more families have rooms
in common-have been rounded up to the next integral number of rooms.
Thus,
three families occupying It, 2!, and 2-l rooms in a 6-roomed house would be
shown as occupying 2, 3, and 3 rooms respectively.
The extent of the rounding-
up process may be ascertained in a general way by reference to table 9, in which
the actual number of rooms is given.
In cases of 1 room occupations, all persons
living therein, whether related or not, have been enumerated as one census family.
FAMILIES.
The term ''Family'' denotes
(a) The i;nhabitants of each institution (see 1 (b) of " Houses" definition),
hotel, boarding-house, lodging-house, or vessel;
(b) Each person or group of persons occupying separately the whole or part
of any dwelling other than as described at (a).
'' Private Family " means a family enumerated in a '' Private House " (see 2
of " Houses" definition).
It should be specially noted that a family or private family for census purposes
includes any group of persons, whether related or not, who live together in
separate house or room accommodation.
RATEABLE VALUATION.
The figures shown are those for service of the year ended 31st March, 1937,
and have been supplied by the Commissioner of Valuation.
Rateable heredita-
ments include lands, buildings, tolls, railways, tramways, canals, fisheries, tur-
baries, mines, quarries, waterworks, telegraphs, telephones, and electricity, gas, and
water mains.
Subject to the provisions of the Local Government (Rating and Finance) Act
(Northern Ireland), 1929, agricultural land and agricultural buildings are com-
pletely derated and industrial hereditaments and freight-transport hereditaments
are partially derated, the consequent deficiency in the yield of rates being made
good by contributions from the Government.


---

## Page 72

viii
The figures in tables ~' 6, and 7 are the totals. of the valuations of the .rateable
hereditaments in the vanous areas before reduction on account of derating, but
exclude in certain cases the valuations of electricity, gas, and water mains, telegraphs,
telephones, :fisheries,
wat~rw?rks, _and tramways. The total of such excluded
valuations for the County IS given In a footnote to the tables.
The total for the County of rateable valuations after reduction on account of
derating is given on page x.
USlTAL RESIDENCE.
The· persons who have been regarded as having their usual residence in the
area of enumeration (i.e., urban district or rural district) include :-
(1) Those enumerated in their own hou,ses.
(2) Persons having settled residence in private lodgings.
(3) Resident domestic servants.
(4)
(5)
Scholars, students, etc., living during term time in residential schools, etc.
Inmates of institutions when the nature of the institution suggested per-
manent residence.
(6) Crews of vessels if usually living on shipboard.
(7) Persons whose usual residence, although not in the building in which
enumerated, is still within the area of enumeration.
All other persons have been treated as usually resident in urban or rural areas
other than the area of enumeration, or in countries outside Northern Ireland, as
stated on the census schedule.
BIRTHPLACES.
The very small number of persons whose birthplaces were not stated on the
census schedule were assumed to have been born in the county in which enumerated
except in the cases of persons with foreign names and persons stated to be visitors
to the household.
After further enquiry in these cases it was found possible to
classify them under definite headings.
RELIGIONS.
The classification· of religions includes a separate heading for each religion, the
adherents of which number 10 or mm;e (see table. 20).
The heading" Presbyterian"
includes those who call th~mselves by this name without qualification, together with
those who describe themselves as members of the Church of Scotland.
Other Presby-
terian denominations are shown at the foot of table 20. Members of the Church of
England and the Episcopal Church of Scotland have been included under the heading
''Church of Irela:n,d.''
The latter group includes all persons returned as, ''Church''
or '' Episcopalian.''
NUMBERING OF TABLES.
Roman numerals are used in this volume for the tables in Part I and Arabic
nuuwrals for the· tables in Part II.
CONVENTION AS TO DOTS (.).AND DASHES (-).
Throughout this volume dots (.) are used in tabular matter to indicate ''none,''
and dashes (-) are used to indicaJe that infcn;matio:o. cannot or is not intended to
. be given.
·
CORRECTION OF CENSUS DATA.
.
Jtvery effort has been made to- eliminate, as far as practi!rnble, errors and
InaccuraCles. from the census data. The process of correction began with the
enu:merators, who received full instructions as to the action to be taken in cases
of error or omission, ~nd it was continued by a system of check and further e:uquiry
where necessar¥ appJ?.ed at ec:ch stage _of the office. work. The process. was com ...
ple~ed by. an lnteusive s~rutiny and Inter-companson of the tabulation sheets.
This scrutmy and co?lPan~on revealed a certain number of incongruities., which,,
when matenal, ~e~e Investigated and corrected in the light of the facts disclosed.'
In a few cases tnfling errors were adjusted arbitrarily.

PART I.
STATISTICAL NOTES.
NoTES.-(1) The definitions, etc., contained ~n the preceding sect·ion apply
equally to these notes and to the tables in Part II.
(2) The descriptions of areas ~tsed in this volu11ze relate to the con-
ditions which existed at the census date.
I1z, particular, the tenn Irish Free State has
been employed in the tables, since the natne Ei·re did not conze into operation for that
area until the 29th December, 1937.
AREA.
The area of Tyrone County, exclusive of the larger rivers and lakes, is 1,218
square miles or 23.2 per cent. of the area of Northern Ireland.
POPULATION.
The total population enumerated in the County was 127,586, and has decreased
by 5,206 or 3.9 per cent. since 1926. A decrease has been recorded at each census
enumeration since 1841 when the population was nearly two and a half times the
present population. (Figures for census years 1821 onwards are shown in table 2).

in population.

* The iigures given relate to each area as constituted at the date of the 1937 Census.
Urban and Rural Districts.-All urban districts, with the exception of
Cookstown, show increases in population during the past eleven years, and all
rural districts show decreases.
In most cases the increase or decrease is a con-
tinuation of the trend since 1901.
The 1937 urban population constitutes 14.6

    per cent. of the whole as compared with 13.3 per cent. in 1926, 12.2 per cent. in
    1911, and 11.3 per cent. in 1901-the percentages quoted relating to urban.districts
as constituted at the date of each census. It will be noted that substantial
increases in population were recorded in Omagh and Strabane Urban Districts,
and that the greatest decreases occurred in the Rural Districts of Omagh, Coqks-
town, and Clog her:
The persons per acre rates vary in the urban districts between 17.5 in Dun-
gannon and 3.0 in Strabane. These rates, however, should be used with reserve
in measuring the density of population in urban districts, since the acreages of
these areas may include widely differing proportions of waste land, land under
pasture, etc.
In rural districts the rates vary between 0.2 and 0.1 persons per
acre.
Table 3 shows the extent to which each urban and rural district in the County
has lost or gained by migration during the intercensal period.
The greatest loss
by n1igration is amongst females in rural districts.
Table 4 provides more detailed information regarding urban and rural districts.
and their constituent district electoral divisions, including figures of rateable valuation.

    The rateable valuation of the County in 1937 was £548.,698 before reduction on
account of derating, and £196,349 after reduction on account of derating.
These
figures compare with a rateable valuation of £468,593 in 1926, but it is to be noted
that during the intercensal period, not only was a derating scheme introduced,..
but a general revaluation took place.

## Page 75

Towns and Villages other than Urban Districts.-Table 5 deals with
towns created under the Towns linprovement (Ireland) Act, 1854, and tovvns or
villages which may be defined as clusters of 50 or more houses with no legally
defin~d boundaries.
The total number of these two classes of towns is 23, and of
these, three have a population of a little over 1,000 each, and five have a population

    of between 500 and 1,000 each. The total population of the 23 tovvns is 11,881.
Unions and Dispensary Districts.-Detailed figures for these areas are given
in table 6.
County Electoral Divisions.-Detailed figures for these, together ·with the
reference numbers of the district electoral divisions comprised in each, are given in
table 7.
Parliamentary Constituencies.-A complete record of the parliamentary
constituencies of the County for elections to both the United Kingdom and Northern
Ireland Parliaments is set out in table 12.
The number of electors as shown therein
is in accordance with the register which came into force on the 15th December, 1937.
This register was compiled on the basis of the qualifying period of six months ending
on 15th July, 1937, and therefore approximates in point of time to the census date.
In general a person is entitled to be registered as a Northern Ireland parlia-
mentary elector if he or she is twenty-one or n1ore years of age, and
(a) has the requisite residence qualification of six months and (unless a native
of N qrthern Ireland) has been resident in the United Kingdom for seven
years prior to the qualifying date ; or
(b) has occupied for six months prior to the qualifying date business premises
of at least £10 valuation; or
(c) is the wife or husband of a person entitled to be registered in respect of a
business premises qualification.
Graduates of full age form the electorate of the constituency of Queen's
University,, Belfast.
The qualifications for voters at elections for the return of members to serve in
the parliament at Westminster are identical with those set out above, except that
the overriding provision as to residence in the United Kingdom does not apply.
In considering the number of electors as shown on the register it should be
noted that a person's name may appear on the register once only on a residence
qualification, but any number of times on a business premises qualification, pro-
vided the residence and business premises are all situated in different constituencies.
Thus a person having a business premises ·vote in each of two constituencies
for Northern Ireland elections would only have one business premises vote for United
Kingdom elections if both business premises were situated in the same United King-
dom constituency.

The intercensal decrease of 260 houses is equivalent to a decrease of less than
1 per cent. as compared with a decrease in population during the eleven years of
almost 4 per cent.
The number of uninhabited houses has also decreased since 1926, and is the
lowest number recorded since 1871.
·
Persons per Inhabited House and Persons per Census Family .-As
1night have been expected, the average number of persons per house, which has

    been steadily declining from 1851, shows a further decrease from 1926 to 1937.
The size of the census family, which shows a corresponding decline, is now a little
over four persons per family.
As compar·ed with a decrease of almost 4 per cent.
in the population since 1926, the number of census families has decreased by only
0.2 per cent.
Ratios for persons per house, persons per family, and persons per room in
relation to inhabited private houses only, can readily be ·calculated for each county
district from tables 8 and 10.

Overcrowding .-In order to determine the measure of accommodation
essential if overcrowding is to be a voided, a knowledge of many factors such as
size of family, number, ages, and sex of children, and number and size of rooms
is necessary.
Moreover, it is a matter of individual judgment as to what weight
should be given to each factor.
In c;Iealing with the accommodation particulars
recorded at the census, the size of the census family has been expressed by the
number of persons, without regard to sex or age, and the rooms comprising the
accommodation have been limited to the usual living rooms.
The resulting rates
of persons per room have been regarded as an approximate measure of density
of room accommodation, and a rate of over 2 persons per room has been assumed
to indicate overcrowding.

Tables VIII and IX have been prepared from table 10. The first of these
tables shows a marked general improvement in accommodation in the County as
a whole, and table IX shows that all county districts, except Dungannon and
Strabane Urban Districts, share in this improvement. The actual amount of

relative degree of overcrowding is indicated by the density rates in the category
of '' over 2 persons per room.''
Housing in Dispensary Districts.-Table 6 contains detailed housing and
accommodation figures for dispensary districts, which show decreases in the number
of houses in 21 out of the 28 districts.
Omagh No. 1 and Strabane Districts are
the only districts which show marked increases, these being due chiefly to the
large increases in the number of houses in Omagh and Strabane Urban Districts
respectively.

The total number and population of hotels and boarding houses in the County
have both decreased by about 25 per cent., while the numbers of inmates of poor
law and other institutions all show small increases.
USUAL RESIDENCE.
The new enquiry at this census as to the "usual residence" of each individual
irrespective of the address at which he or she was enumerated has disclosed that
only l.l per cent. of the enumerated population of County Tyrone was normally-
resident elsewhere than in the urban or rural district of enumeration, and more
than one-third of these resided outside Northern Ireland.
Detailed figures are
contained in table 13.
This table shows the effect of an adjustment of
the enumerated population for usual residence as between the urban or rural
district of enumeration and other such areas in Northern Ireland.
While this.
adjustment is comparatively insignificant, it may be noted that it would
* Table X (Housing in Dispensary Districts) is given for Belfast and Londonderry County Boroughs only.


---

## Page 80

xvi
probably have been larger if the census had been taken some months later. More-
·over, the 1937 population figures adjusted for usual residence as at the end of
February -will provide better comparisons with population figures similarly adjusted
at a future census (which may be taken at some other time of .the year) than
simple comparisons of populations as enumerated. In the absence of particulars
of persons not enumerated in Northern Ireland, but usually resident in the County,
it is not possible to make a similar adjustment for usual residence as between areas
in the County and places outside Northern Ireland.
If, however, a census
·enumeration were carried out on the same date throughout the British Isles, it
could be made to provide most of the particulars required to give effect to such
an adjustment.

BIRTHPLACES.
Table 18 sho·ws, by sex, the numbers of persons born in each of the counties
of Northern Ireland, other countries in the British Isles, overseas dominions,
colonies, etc., foreign countries, and at sea.
Of the total population of Tyrone
County, 84 per cent. were born in the County, 8 per cent. in the remainder of
Northern Ireland, and 5 per cent. in the Irish Free State.
Only 0.6 per cent. were
born outside the British Isles, and more than one third of these were born in
British Dominions and Colonies.
Persons born in foreign countries numbered
483, and 9 persons were born at sea.

## Page 84

XX
From table XVIII it will be seen that since 1861 there have been no marked
variations in the proportions of the population born in the several parts of. the
British Isles and abroad. Up to 1926 the general tendency was for the proportions
-born in Northern Ireland to fall and the proportions born in other parts of the
British Isles and abroad to rise.
This tendency was, however, checked in the
last intercensal period when there was a reduction in the proportions born in
Great Britain and in the Irish Free State.
Table 19 deals with the numbers of persons in each county district born
outside Northern Ireland and shows them under Roman Catholic and other
denominations, by sex, and duration of residence in Northern Ireland. The
irregularities in the run of the figures as between males and females in some
cases are no doubt due to circumstances peculiar to particular districts.
Districts with a high proportion of domestic servants, and those which include
schools, institutions, etc., for example, might be expected to show some exceptional
features.

r
Table XIX has been prepared from table 19 and shows under Roman Catholic
and other denominations the numbers of persons who have come from
other countries to reside in the County, and were still there on census day. It will
be seen that the ratio of such persons to the total population of the same denomina-
tion for all periods of residence does not differ widely as between Roman Catholics
and persons of other denominations, although, for the shorter durations of residence,
the proportions of persons of other denominations are the greater. The differences
in the ratios, however, do not suggest that in recent years there has been any
disproportionate influx of persons, either of Roman Catholic or other deno1ninations
from other countries into the County, unless any such influx has been offset by a
corresponding movement outwards from the County.
RELIGIONS.
Table 20 shows th~ P?Pulation by sex and religion in four main groups for
each urban and rural d1stnct, and by sex and under twenty-one different descrip-
tions for the County as a whole. The highest proportions of Roman Catholics are
to be found in Strabane and Omagh Urban Districts, and in Omagh Rural District.

* Including Church of England, numbering 500 in 1937.
From table XX it will be seen that there has been a decrease both in the
number and percentage of Roman Catholics, and that, although the numbers of
Presbyterians, Church of Ireland, and Methodists, all show small decreases, their
proportions are the same as in 1926. A slight increase both in the number and
percentage of persons of other denominations has been recorded.
During the
period from 1861 onwards the percentage of persons of other denominations has
shown a tendency to increase, but the percentages of other religions have not
fluctuated to any appreciable extent.

## Page 121

PREFACE.
This volume has been prepared in accordance \vith the provisions of Section
6 (I) ·of the Census Act (Northern Ireland), 1936, from the census returns made
as at midnight of the 28th February-1st March, 1937.
The statistics contained
herein supersede those in the Preliminary Report published in July, 1937, and
may be regarded as final.
The census which was taken in 1937 was a special one in the sense that it
was limited in scope and was taken during the interval between normal census
years, thus bridging the gap between the 1926 census and the next full census.
Certain questions usually found on census schedules, such as those regarding
occupation and industry, were not asked, but a new question as to usual residence
was included for the first time.
The 1937 census publications will consist of:-
1.
SEVEN CouNTY VoLUMES, each similar in design and scope.
The smallest
area for which statistics are given in the county volumes is the district
electoral division, but statistics, in respect of townlands similar to those
given in table 4 have been prepared in the Census Office and are
available for reference upon application to the Registrar General.
2.
GENERAL REPORT for the whole country, covering in more detail the
statistics shown in the county volumes, and in ad¢l.ition containing
tables on fertility.
3.
SuPPLEMENT TO 1926 ToPOGRAPHICAL INDEX.
This supplement will be
designed for use in conjunction with the 1926 topographical index in
order to facilitate reference to the statistics given in the 1937 census
reports.
It will cover the topographical changes during the intercensal
period.
The net prices for the 1937 census publications will be 2s. 6d. for each
county volume, 5s. Od. for the general report, and Is. Od. for the topographical
index supplement.
The remaining county volumes will be published at intervals as completed
during the next six months, and the other two volumes as soon as possible after
that period.
These publications will be obtainable directly from the Superin-
tendent, H.M. Stationery Office, 80 Chichester Street, Belfast, or through any
bookseller.
The 1926 census publications are similar in arrangement to the 1937 series
(see advertisement on back of cover), but they incorporate additional tables on
occupations, industries, education, orphanhood, dependent children, infirmities,
and nationality. In addition the 1926 county volumes contain population and
housing figures for each townland and village.
The Registrar General is empowered by Section 6 (2) of the Census Act (Nor-
thern Ireland), 1936, to prepare and supply from the census returns, statistics
other than those published in the census reports at the request and cost of any
local authority or person.
Any special demands for statistics more detailed than
those published (either as regards subject matter or area) can thusJ as far as office
records permit, be satisfied.
Requests for special compilation of census statistics
should be addressed to the Registrar General, who will be pleased to advise and
furnish an estimate of cost.
}ULY, 1938.
MINISTRY OF FINANCE,
GENERAL REGISTER OFFICE,
FERMANAGH HOUSE,
0RMEAU AVENUE, BELFAST.

## Page 124

EXPLANATORY NOTES AND DEFINITIONS OF
TERMS USED IN THIS VOLUME.
AREA.
The acreages given are as measured by the O~d?anc~ Survey of 1930. (fore-
. shore being excluded), and relate to the several adm1n1stratlve areas .as cor:stltuted
at the date of the 1937 census.
The ac~eage covered by tidal water 1s not Included
in the body of Tables 4 and 6, but is given in footnotes thereto.
POPULATION.
The population comprises all persons, including Forces of the Crown, enume-
rated as at midnight of Sunday, 28th February-1st March, 1937.
The population assigned to each area. comprises all persons who were actually
present within it, whether as residents or visitors, persons on board ship in port
being included in the area adjacent to the port.
In general these definitions apply to the population figures for previous census
enumerations which have been included in some of the tables.
ADMINISTRATIVE DIVISIONS.
The County Borough is divided into fifteen wards, which form units for the
purpose of borough council elections, and each ward is coterminous with the
district electoral division of the same nan1e (see table 4).
The district electoral
division forms the unit for the purpose of poor law elections.
The Poor Law Union of Belfast consists of the County Borough, Belfast and
Castlereagh Rural Districts and Holywood Urban District.
The part of the Poor
Law Union within the County Borough is divided into sixteen medical dispensary
districts. Poor law unions and medical dispensary districts are also, respectively,
superintendent registrars' and registrars' districts for the registration of marriages,
births, and deaths.
The County Borough is divided into sixteen parliamentary constituencies for
elections to the Northern Ireland Parliament, and into four constituencies for
elections to the United Kingdom Parliament (see table 12).
There are no county electoral divisions in the County Borough.
(County
electoral divisions are the areas into which counties are divided for the purpose
of county council elections).
The Belfast Education Area is coterminous with the County Borough.
The only change made in the boundaries of administrative divisions of the
County Borough during the last intercensal period was that caused by the creation
by the House of Commons (Method of Voting and Redistribution of Seats) Act
(Northern Ireland), 1929, of sixteen parliamentary constituencies for the purpose
of elections to the Parliament of Northern Ireland.
HOUSES.
l. The term '' House '' denotes
(a) each structure containing dwelling accommodation (except institutions)
which stands alone or is separated from another by a vertical parting
wall;
(b) each institution (viz., hospital, sanatorium, nursing home, infirmary,
workhouse, asylum, religious or charitable community, residential
school, barracks or other premises under Naval, Military, Air Force,
or Police discipline, prison or other place of detention), regardless of
the number of buildings within its precincts ;
(c) each vessel, caravan, tent, or other movable dwelling.

vii
2.
The term " Private House" denotes all " Houses" other than institu-
tions, hotels, boarding houses, premises occupied by caretakers, vessels, movable
dwellings, and barns, stables, and out-houses occupied on census night. The
term includes premises comprising a shop and dwelling, unless occupied by a
caretaker.
3.
The terms " Boarding House " and " Lodging House " cover habitations
described as such, and any private house containing three or more boarders or
lodgers.
4.
The term " Inhabited" denotes actual residence of a person or persons
on census night.
" Uninhabited " houses, therefore, include those temporarily
vacant through the absence of the usual occupiers as well as those without tenants.
Enumerators were instructed to omit from their enumeration empty houses in
such an advanced state of decay that there was little likelihood that they ·would
again be made fit for habitation.
ROOMS.
1.
The rooms enumerated are the usual living rooms, including bedrooms
and kitchens, but excluding sculleries (i.e., rooms used only for washing, cleaning,
or cooking), pantries, cloakrooms, landings, closets or bathrooms, or any ware-
house, office or shop rooms.
2.
For the purpose of classifying families by rooms occupied (Table 10),
shared-room occupations-i.e., those in which two or more families have rooms
in common-have been rounded up to the next integral number of rooms.
Thus,
three families occupying li, 21, and 2l rooms in a 6-roomed house would
be shown as occupying 2, 3, and 3 rooms respectively. The extent of the rounding-
up process may be ascertained in a general way by reference to Table 9, in which
the actual number of rooms is given.
In cases of 1 room occupations, all persons
living therein, whether related or not, have been enumerated as one census family.
FAMILIES.
The term "Family" denotes
(a) The inhabitants of each institution (see 1 (b) of" Houses" definition),
hotel, boarding-house, lodging-house, or ship.
(b) Each person or group of persons occupying separately the whole, or part
of, any dwelling other than as described at (a).
cc Private Family " means a family enumerated in a " Private House " (see
2 of '' Houses '' definition).
It should be specially noted that a family or private family for census purposes
includes any group of persons, whether related or not, vvho live together in
separate house or room accommodation.
RATEABLE VALUATION.
The figures shown are those for service of the year ended 31st March, 1937,
and have been supplied by the Commissioner of Valuation.
Rateable Heredita-
ments include lands, buildings, tolls, railways, tramways, canals, fisheries, tur-
baries, mines, quarries, waterworks, telegraphs, telephones and electricity, gas and
water mains.
Subject to the provisions of the Local Government (Rating and Finance) Act
(Northern Ireland), 1929, agricultural land and agricultural buildings are com-
pletely derated and industrial hereditaments and freight-transport hereditaments
are partially derated, the consequent deficiency in the yield of rates being made
good by contributions from the Government.
The figures in Table 4 are the totals of the valuations of the rateable heredita-
ments in the various areas before reduction on account of derating, but exclude
in certain cases the valuations of electricity, gas and water mains, telegraphs,
telephones, fisheries, waterworks and tramways.
The total of such excluded
valuations for the county is given in a footnote to the table.


---

## Page 126

viii
The total for the County Borough of rateable valuations after reduction on
account of derating is given on page xi.
USUAL RESIDENCE.
The persons -vvho have been regarded as .having their usual residence in the area
of enumeration (i.e., the County Borough) 1nclude :-
(1) Those enumerated in their own houses.
(2) Persons having settled residence in private lodgings.
(3) Resident domestic servants.
(4) Scholars, students, etc., living during term time in residential schools, etc.
(5) Inmates of institutions when the nature of the institution suggested per ...
manent residence.
(6) Crews of vessels if usually living on shipboard.
(7) Persons whose usual residence, although not in the building in which
enumerated, is still within the area of enumeration.
All other persons have been treated as usually resident in urban and rural
areas other than the area of enumeration, or in countries outside Northern Ireland,
as stated on the census schedule.
BIRTHPLACES.
Persons whose birthplaces were not stated on the census schedule were assumed
to have been born in the county in which enumerated, except in cases of persons
with foreign names, and persons stated to be visitors to the household. In these
cases further enquiries were made.
RELIGIONS.
The classification of religions includes a separate heading for each religion,
the adherents of which number 10 or more (see Table 20).
The heading'' Presby-
terian " includes those who call themselves by this name without qualification,
together with those who describe themselves as members of the Church of Scotland.
Other Presbyterian denominations are sho-vvn at the foot of Table 20.
Members
of the Church of England and the Episcopal Church of Scotland have been in-
cluded under the heading " Church of Ireland."
The latter group includes all
persons returned as " Church" or " Episcopalian."
NUMBERING OF TABLES.
Roman numerals are used in this volume for the tables in Part I and Arabic
numerals for the tables in Part II.
CONVENTION AS TO DOTS (.) AND DASHES (-)·
Throughout this volume dots (.)are used in tabular matter to indicate "none,"
and dashes (-) are used to indicate that information cannot or is not intended to
be given.
CORRECTION OF CENSUS DATA.
Evert effort has been made to eliminate, as far as practicable, errors and
inaccuracies from the census data. The process of correction began with the
enumerators, who received full instructions as to the action to be laken in cases
of error or omission, and it -vvas continued by a system of check and further enquiry
where necessary applied at each stage of the office ·work.
The process was com-
pleted by an intensive scrutiny and inter-comparison of the tabulation sheets.
This scrutiny and comparison revealed a certain number of incongruities, which,
when material, were investigated and corrected in the light of the facts disclosed.
In a few cases trifling errors were adjusted arbitrarily.

PART I.
STATISTICAL NOTES.
NOTES.-(1) The definitions, etc., contained in the preceding sect·ion apply
equally to these ·notes and to the tables in Part II.
(2) The descriptions of areas used in this volunze relate to the con-
ditions which existed at the censzts date.
In particular, the ternt Irish Free State has
been employed in the tables, since the nan~e E·ire did not conz.e in-to operation for that
area t<tntil the 29th December, 1937.
AREA.
The area of the County Borough of Belfast, which in 1851 \vas roughly 2l
square miles, now comprises (as a result of boundary extensions in 1853 and 1896)
about 23 square miles, exclusive of tidal water. It \vill be seen from Table 1 ·
(Part II) that some 13t square miles are occupied by buildings, roads, etc., and
more than 9 square miles are under crops and pasture.
POPULATION.
The total population enumerated in the County Borough \Vas 438,086.
This
figure is more than twice the number enumerated in 1881 and more than six times
the number enumerated in 1841.
The increase of 22,935 in the population during
the past eleven years is equivalent to an increase of 0.5 per cent. per annum, this
increase being only slightly higher than the average annual increase of the preceding
intercensal period, which was the lowest recorded since 1851.

*Owing to the extension of the County Borough boundary in 1896, it is not possible "to show figures in respect
of the area which now constitutes the County Borough for periods prior to 1901.
·
t Including the deaths of non-civilians of the County Borough. which occurred outside Northern Ireland.
Although comparable figures of natural increase due to the excess of births
over deaths, in respect of the County Borough as now constituted, are not available

    for periods prior to 1901, it is evident that during the period 1821 to 1901 the
increase in population due to excess of births over deaths was considerably aug-
mented by net movement into the city.
For the period 1901-1911 a small net
movement in the opposite direction was recorded, and during the last twenty-six
years the net movement has continued in an outward direction, the dimensions
of this movement being such as to offset materially the increase in population
which would otherwise have been experienced from the excess of births over deaths.
It will be seen from Table 3 that during the period 1926-1937 the loss by migration
consisted mainly of males. The present trend of population in Belfast and in the
urban and rural districts within easy reach of Belfast indicates an increasing
tendency for city workers to live either in the suburbs or country, or at the seaside.

Wards.-Eight of the fifteen wards of Belfast show increases of population
during the past el~ven years, and seven show decreases.
The more remarkable ·
increases have taken place in Clift9n and St. Anne's Wards, the population of each
of these wards having increased by about 35 per cent. since 1926. The greatest
decreases have occurred in the four wards having the four highest numbers of
persons per acre. In order of rate of decrease they are St. George's (with the
highest rate of decrease, viz., 16.9 per cent.), Dock, Smithfield, and Court Wards.
No marked decreases of population were apparent in any of these wards in the
intercensal periods prior to the 1926 census, except in the case of Smithfield Ward, ·
the population of which decreased by about 12 per cent. during the period
1901-1911.

    of £1,_793,563 in 1926, .but it is to be noted that during the intercensal period a
derating scheme was Introduced and a general revaluation took place.

Dispensary Districts.-The dispensary districts of Belfast vary in size con-
siderably, and their boundaries differ from the ward boundaries in all cases,
except in that of District No. 11 which is coterminous with Ormeau W..ard.
Of
the sixteen dispensary districts, eight show increases of population during the past
eleven years, and eight show decreases.
The most substantial increases occurred

    in Districts Nos. 8, 9, and 13, and the greatest decreases in Districts Nos. 3, 15,
and 16.
The increase in the population of Belfast during the period 1911-1926
was shared more or less equally by all districts, but the increase in the past eleven
years is the net result of increases in one half of the dispensary districts partly
offset by substantial decreases in the other half.
More detailed information re-
garding dispensary districts is given in table 6.
Parliamentary Constituencies.-A complete record of the parliamentary
constituencies of Belfast for elections to both the United Kingdom and Northern
Ireland Parliaments is set out in table 12. The number of electors as shown
therein is in accordance with the register which came into force on the 15th


---

## Page 130

xu
December, 1937.
This register was compiled on the basis of th~ qualif:>;ing _12eriod
of six months ending on 15th July, 1937, and therefore approximates 1n po1nt of
tiine to the census date.
In general a person is entitled to be registered as a Northern Ireland parlia-
mentary elector if he or she is twenty-one or more years of age, and
(a) has the requisite residence qualification of six months and (unless a native
of Northern Ireland) has been resident in the United Kingdom for seven
years prior to the qualifying date; or
(b) has occupied for six months prior to the qualifying date business premises
of at least £10 valuation ; or
(c) is the ·wife or husband of a person entitled to be registered in respect of
a business premises qualification.
Graduates of full age form the electorate of the University constituency.
The qualifications for voters at elections for the return of members to serve
in the Parliament at Westminster are identical with those set out above, except
that the overriding provision as to residence in the United Kingdom does not apply.
In considering the number of electors as shown on the register it should be
noted that a person's name may appear on the register once only on a residence
qualification, but any number of times on a business premises qualification, pro-
vided the residence and business premises are all situated in different constituencies.
Thus a person having a business premises vote in each of two constituencies for
Northern Ireland elections would only have one business premises vote for United
Kingdom elections if both business premises were situated in the same United
Kingdom constituency.

*The figures given are for the Borough as constituted at the date of each Census. The boundary of the Borough
was extended in 1853 and again in 1896.
The intercensal increase of 21,868 houses is equivalent to an increase of nearly
26 per cent., as compared with an increase in population over the eleven years
of 5! per cent.
The number of uninhabited houses has increased materially since 1926, but,·
compared with the numbers recorded at previous censuses, the 1937 figure is not
abnormally high.
Persons per Inhabited House and Persons per Census Family .-As
might have been expected, the average number of persons per house, which has
been steadily declining from 1851, shows a further and marked fall from 1926 to
1937.
The size of the census family, which first showed a tendency to decrease in
1926, has also further decreased, and now stands at a little over four persons per
family.
As compared with the 5! per cent. increase in the population since 1926,
the number of census families has increased by 17! per cent.
Ratios for persons per house, persons per family, and persons per room in
relation to inhabited private houses only, can readily be calculated for each ward
from tables 8 and 10.

of the County Borough is as follows :-
